###begin article-title 0
###xml 29 53 29 53 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 29 53 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
The YEATS domain of Taf14 in Saccharomyces cerevisiae has a negative impact on cell growth
###end article-title 0
###begin p 1
Communicated by M. Collart.
###end p 1
###begin p 2
###xml 102 126 102 126 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 131 144 131 144 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 1136 1141 1136 1141 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14</italic>
###xml 1197 1201 1197 1201 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DST1</italic>
###xml 102 126 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 131 144 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
The role of a highly conserved YEATS protein motif is explored in the context of the Taf14 protein of Saccharomyces cerevisiae. In S. cerevisiae, Taf14 is a protein physically associated with many critical multisubunit complexes including the general transcription factors TFIID and TFIIF, the chromatin remodeling complexes SWI/SNF, Ino80 and RSC, Mediator and the histone modification enzyme NuA3. Taf14 is a member of the YEATS superfamily, conserved from bacteria to eukaryotes and thought to have a transcription stimulatory activity. However, besides its ubiquitous presence and its links with transcription, little is known about Taf14's role in the nucleus. We use structure-function and mutational analysis to study the function of Taf14 and its well conserved N-terminal YEATS domain. We show here that the YEATS domain is not necessary for Taf14's association with these transcription and chromatin remodeling complexes, and that its presence in these complexes is dependent only on its C-terminal domain. Our results also indicate that Taf14's YEATS domain is not necessary for complementing the synthetic lethality between TAF14 and the general transcription factor TFIIS (encoded by DST1). Furthermore, we present evidence that the YEATS domain of Taf14 has a negative impact on cell growth: its absence enables cells to grow better than wild-type cells under stress conditions, like the microtubule destabilizing drug benomyl. Moreover, cells expressing solely the YEATS domain grow worser than cells expressing any other Taf14 construct tested, including the deletion mutant. Thus, this highly conserved domain should be considered part of a negative regulatory loop in cell growth.
###end p 2

###begin title 5
Keywords
###end title 5
###begin title 6
Introduction
###end title 6
###begin p 7
###xml 563 567 563 567 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR60">2006</xref>
RNA polymerase II transcription is a tightly regulated process in which the chromatin near the gene to be transcribed is re-organized and remodeled, followed by the formation of the transcription pre-initiation complex and the processes of initiation, elongation and termination. Different multi-protein complexes are involved in these processes for regulation under normal or stress conditions. A similar phenomenon of complex regulation occurs during cell cycle progression where many changes take place including modulation of the cytoskeleton (Sawin and Tran 2006). Taf14 is one of the few proteins thought to play a role in all of these vital processes but its specific role is still unknown, and it is the focus of this work.
###end p 7

TAF14 was first identified (as ANC1) in a screen for mutations that enhance defects caused by a mutation in the actin gene (Welch et al. 1993). Cells devoid of Taf14 are viable but thermo- and osmo-sensitive (Henry et al. 1994) have defects in actin organization (Welch et al. 1993), and aberrant morphology and cytoskeletal defects (Henry et al. 1994). Additionally, Taf14 is physically associated with a variety of transcription and chromatin remodeling complexes (Fig. 1a). It is present in, at least, three different ATP-dependent chromatin remodeling complexes: Swi/Snf, which possesses DNA-stimulated ATPase activity and activates transcription by helping transcription factors access their binding sites (Cairns et al. 1996; Sudarsanam and Winston 2000; Dechassa et al. 2008); Ino80, which has helicase activity and is involved in transcription, replication and repair (Shen et al. 2003a, b; Conaway and Conaway 2009); and RSC, essential for cell cycle progression (Du et al. 1998; Eberharter et al. 1998), and known for repositioning and ejecting nucleosomes from active sites (Parnell et al. 2008). Taf14 also has been found in the histone-acetyltransferase complex, NuA3 (John et al. 2000; Kabani et al. 2005) that stimulates transcription or replication elongation through nucleosomes by providing a coupled acetyltransferase activity (John et al. 2000). All of these complexes help transcriptional activators bind to occluded chromatin sites by perturbing histone-DNA contacts or by covalently modifying histones (Clark-Adams et al. 1988; Imbalzano et al. 1994; Steger et al. 1998; Shen et al. 2000; Neely and Workman 2002; Cairns 2005). Roles for these complexes were first identified in transcriptional regulation (Kingston and Narlikar 1999; Davie and Kane 2000; Narlikar et al. 2002), but it has recently been shown that they also play diverse and complex roles in the DNA damage response (for review (Costelloe et al. 2006; Park et al. 2009)).Fig. 1a Seven nuclear complexes that contain or bind Taf14. The sizes are not to scale. The names of the proteins tagged in this study from each of the complexes are shown. b Truncated versions of Taf14 used in this study: TAF14, taf14[YEATS] (amino acids 6-113), taf14[CT] (amino acids 113-244), and taf14[-NT] (amino acids 61-244). c Expression of the different Taf14 constructs. Equal amounts of cell extract were resolved on a 10% SDS-Laemmli protein gel and transferred to a nitrocellulose membrane for western blotting. Taf14 proteins were detected with Polyclonal Taf14 antibody. Left paneltaf14Delta strain (CMKy70) containing plasmids expressing Taf14 constructs under its natural promoter. The YEATS fragment (Y second line) is practically not detected. Empty plasmid was used as negative control, PGK protein as loading control. Right paneltaf14Delta strain (CMKy70) containing high copy plasmids expressing Tap-tagged Taf14 constructs under the PGK promoter. Empty pVV233 vector (TAP tag alone) was used as the negative control. The TAP tag is visible with the Taf14 antibody because it contains Protein A, recognized by IgG's in every polyclonal antibody. H+ vacuolar ATPase was used as loading control
###end p 8

###xml 78 82 78 82 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR56">1995</xref>

###xml 128 132 128 132 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR5">1996</xref>
###xml 188 192 188 192 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR37">2004</xref>
###xml 347 351 347 351 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR48">1996</xref>
###xml 365 369 365 369 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR7">2009</xref>
###xml 491 495 491 495 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR3">1989</xref>
###xml 511 515 511 515 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR22">1991</xref>
###xml 517 521 517 521 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR23">1992</xref>
###xml 536 540 536 540 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">1994</xref>
###xml 602 606 602 606 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR58">1998</xref>
###xml 624 628 624 628 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR45">2005</xref>
###xml 648 652 648 652 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">2008</xref>
###xml 684 688 684 688 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR58">1998</xref>
###xml 705 709 705 709 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR9">2000</xref>
###xml 724 728 724 728 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR78">2005</xref>
###xml 805 809 805 809 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2005</xref>
###xml 911 915 911 915 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR36">1994</xref>
###xml 931 935 931 935 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">2008</xref>
###xml 1135 1139 1135 1139 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR50">2000</xref>
###xml 1153 1157 1153 1157 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR2">2006</xref>
###xml 1169 1173 1169 1173 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR29">2008</xref>
###xml 1226 1230 1226 1230 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">ADH1</italic>
###xml 1251 1255 1251 1255 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR40">2002</xref>
###xml 1265 1269 1265 1269 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">GAL1</italic>
###xml 1290 1294 1290 1294 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2005</xref>
###xml 31 36 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Taf14 is also a subunit of the yeast transcription factors TFIID (Poon et al. 1995) and TFIIF (Henry et al. 1994; Cairns et al. 1996) and seems to interact with TFIIB (Kimura and Ishihama 2004). TFIID recognizes the TATA box or other promoter elements and enables the binding of TFIIB and TFIIA. It is also known to modify histones (Mizzen et al. 1996; Cler et al. 2009). TFIIF assists the recruitment of RNA polymerase II, TFIIE and TFIIH into the pre-initiation complex (Buratowski et al. 1989; Flores et al. 1991, 1992; Henry et al. 1994). It is also involved in start site selection (Robert et al. 1998; Majovski et al. 2005; Khaperskyy et al. 2008) and elongation (Robert et al. 1998; Conaway et al. 2000; Zhang et al. 2005). Although a direct interaction has not always been detected (Kabani et al. 2005), Taf14 has also been suggested as a component of the transcriptional activator Mediator (Kim et al. 1994; Erlich et al. 2008), a global regulator important in recruiting of the transcription machinery and modulating RNA polymerase II activity after the alteration of the chromatin template by remodelers (Myers and Kornberg 2000; Baek et al. 2006; He et al. 2008). Supporting a role for Taf14 in initiation, in the ADH1 gene (Krogan et al. 2002) and the GAL1 gene (Kabani et al. 2005), Taf14 is recruited to the promoter region and not to the coding region, suggesting that Taf14 may be involved in the assembly of the RNA polymerase II pre-initiation complex. This recruitment is likely due to its presence in TFIID and TFIIF, but Taf14 also could have been recruited to that region through its association with the chromatin remodeling complexes described above.
###end p 10
###begin p 11
###xml 91 115 91 115 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 227 231 227 231 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">2006</xref>
###xml 451 455 451 455 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR20">2002</xref>
###xml 475 479 475 479 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR25">2008</xref>
###xml 589 593 589 593 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">2000</xref>
###xml 607 611 607 611 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">2006</xref>
###xml 811 815 811 815 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">2006</xref>
###xml 960 964 960 964 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DST1</italic>
###xml 1041 1045 1041 1045 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR46">2004</xref>
###xml 1048 1052 1048 1052 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">SOH1</italic>
###xml 1210 1214 1210 1214 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR18">1996</xref>
###xml 1281 1285 1281 1285 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR41">2003</xref>
###xml 1464 1474 1464 1470 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">cdc40&#916;</italic>
###xml 1499 1503 1495 1499 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR12">2004</xref>
###xml 91 115 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1346 1351 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
In addition to the roles of Taf14 in chromatin remodeling and transcription initiation, in Saccharomyces cerevisiae there is a strong genetic interaction between Taf14 and the transcription elongation factor TFIIS (Fish et al. 2006). TFIIS stimulates RNA polymerase II during elongation to recover from an arrested state by inducing the polymerase to cleave its nascent transcript, thus realigning the new RNA 3' end in the active site (Fish and Kane 2002; Ghavi-Helm et al. 2008). Neither TFIIS nor Taf14 is essential, but the double mutant results in synthetic lethality (Davie and Kane 2000; Fish et al. 2006). These two proteins are not functionally redundant, but have a functional interaction within a cellular process, most likely during transcriptional elongation and in concert with TFIIF (Fish et al. 2006). Taf14 (as well as proteins Gal1, Sua7, Rts1 or Tys1) has additionally been reported as a strong suppressor of the genetic interaction between DST1 (the gene encoding for TFIIS) and SOH1 (when overexpressed) (Malagon et al. 2004). SOH1 is a member of Mediator and, in Saccharomyces, is synthetically lethal with mutations in genes encoding subunits of the RNA polymerase II, TFIIB (Fan et al. 1996) and the histone H3 methyl transferase factor Set2 (Krogan et al. 2003). Taf14 is also one of the 5% of intron containing genes in yeast and, surprisingly, splicing of its intron is necessary to suppress a temperature-sensitive cell cycle arrest of cdc40Delta cells (Dahan and Kupiec 2004).
###end p 11
###begin p 12
###xml 275 279 275 279 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR28">2000</xref>
###xml 427 431 427 431 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR61">2009</xref>
###xml 496 507 496 507 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Arabidopsis</italic>
###xml 509 519 509 519 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Drosophila</italic>
###xml 521 544 521 544 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Caenorhabditis elegans,</italic>
###xml 587 591 587 591 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR10">1996</xref>
###xml 619 623 619 623 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR19">1997</xref>
###xml 868 872 868 872 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR1">1999</xref>
###xml 948 952 948 952 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR49">2001</xref>
###xml 970 974 970 974 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR53">2006</xref>
###xml 1262 1266 1262 1266 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR44">2003</xref>
###xml 1281 1285 1281 1285 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR77">2004</xref>
###xml 1403 1407 1403 1407 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR69">2003</xref>
###xml 1421 1425 1421 1425 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR65">2005</xref>
###xml 131 136 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 496 507 <span type="species:ncbi:3702">Arabidopsis</span>
###xml 509 519 <span type="species:ncbi:7227">Drosophila</span>
###xml 521 543 <span type="species:ncbi:6239">Caenorhabditis elegans</span>
###xml 545 550 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 555 560 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 658 663 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
###xml 1062 1067 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1125 1130 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
Taf14 contains a highly conserved YEATS domain at its N-terminus and along with Yaf9 and Sas5 comprises the YEATS domain family in yeast. The name for this domain derives from the first discovered proteins containing the domain (Yaf9, ENL, AF9, Taf14, Sas5) (Harborth et al. 2000). Its function has not yet been described, but it is thought to be implicated in regulating transcription and chromatin remodeling (Schulze et al. 2009). This domain is found in many eukaryotic species as diverse as Arabidopsis, Drosophila, Caenorhabditis elegans, yeast and human. ENL, AF-9 (Corral et al. 1996) and Gas41 (Fischer et al. 1997) are the most extensively studied human proteins in this group. These three proteins are directly implicated in leukemias and gliomas whether by fusion of their non-YEATS domain to a protein called MLL in the case of ENL and AF-9 (Adler et al. 1999) or by amplification of its expression in the case of Gas41 (Munnia et al. 2001; Park and Roeder 2006). As mentioned above, the Taf14 YEATS domain presents striking homology with two other yeast nuclear proteins, Yaf9 and Sas5. Yaf9 is the ortholog of human Gas41, and is a component of the NuA4 HAT complex and the SWR1 complex, which deposits the histone variant H2A.Z (Le Masson et al. 2003; Zhang et al. 2004). Sas5 is a component of the SAS complex involved in chromatin silencing by acetylating free histones (Sutton et al. 2003; Shia et al. 2005).
###end p 12
###begin p 13
Here, we present the first evidence that the YEATS domain of Taf14 exerts a negative effect on cell growth, and deletion of the YEATS domain results in a growth advantage under certain stress conditions. We further show that the C-terminus but not the YEATS domain of Taf14 is necessary and sufficient for the rescue of the synthetic lethality with TFIIS. Moreover, the C-terminus is also sufficient for association of Taf14 with the complexes involved in transcription and chromatin remodeling described above, revealing that the YEATS domain is not required for interaction with these complexes. These results suggest that, rather than a structural role, the YEATS domain has a regulatory function, possibly coordinating the transcription and replication processes during the cell cycle.
###end p 13

###xml 4 17 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 285 301 <span type="species:ncbi:562">Escherichia coli</span>
###xml 367 374 <span type="species:ncbi:562">E. coli</span>
###xml 383 388 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
The S. cerevisiae strains and plasmids used in this study are listed in Tables 1 and 2. Taf14 point mutations were generated based on oligonucleotide-directed mutagenesis (Kunkel et al. 1987) using the "Quick Change" mutagenesis strategy, as directed by the manufacturer (Stratagene). Escherichia coli calcium-manganese-based transformations were used with the Top10 E. coli strain. ###begin p 17
Plasmids used in this study
###end p 17
###begin p 18
###xml 0 5 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">Yeast</span>
Yeast strains used in this study
###end p 18
###begin p 19
###xml 108 112 108 112 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR27">1991</xref>
Media such as YPD or synthetic complete (SC) were prepared according to standard procedures (Guthrie et al. 1991). All chemicals were from Sigma-Aldrich unless otherwise noted. SE media was prepared as SC but by substituting the nitrogen source with 1 g/L of glutamic acid (monosodium salt). G418 Sulfate was added to autoclaved YPD or SE media at a concentration of 200 mug/mL. ClonNAT (WERNER BioAgents) was added to autoclaved media at 100 mug/mL. 6-Azauracil (6-AU) was added to autoclaved media at a final concentration of 60 mug/mL; NaCl, KCl, and sorbitol were included at a final concentration of 0.9, 1.2 and 1.8 M, respectively. Benomyl was added immediately after autoclaving media at a concentration of 35 mug/mL, from a stock solution at 10 mg/mL in dimethyl sulfoxide.
###end p 19
###begin p 20
###xml 82 85 82 85 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
###xml 385 388 379 382 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
###xml 534 537 522 525 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
###xml 557 558 545 546 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">R</italic>
###xml 559 561 547 549 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2P</sup>
For the growth studies on plates, cells in exponential phase were diluted to an OD600 of 0.1 and four serial 10x dilutions were made. The same volume of cells was applied onto YPD or SC-URA plates with or without additions as indicated in the text. Cells were grown at 24, 30, or 36degreesC. For the growth studies in liquid media, cells in early stationary phase were diluted to an OD600 of 0.05 and grown at 30degreesC. Samples were taken every 1 h. The mean of the doubling times was calculated with data from exponential phase (OD600 0.18-2.0). All the RP2P were larger than 0.9.
###end p 20
###begin title 21
Strain recovery and tetrad analysis
###end title 21
###begin p 22
###xml 23 28 23 28 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14</italic>
###xml 85 104 85 96 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;dst1&#916;</italic>
###xml 197 218 189 206 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14/taf14&#916;::kan</italic>
###xml 218 219 206 207 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">r</italic>
###xml 218 219 206 207 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>r</italic></sup>
###xml 219 252 207 232 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">, dst1&#916;::hisG/dst1&#916;::hisG</italic>
###xml 295 300 275 280 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14</italic>
The ability of various TAF14 constructs to complement the synthetic lethality in the taf14Deltadst1Delta strain was determined by tetrad analysis of heterozygous diploids. After sporulation of the TAF14/taf14Delta::kanr, dst1Delta::hisG/dst1Delta::hisG diploid (CMKy91) containing the different TAF14 constructs on a URA-marked plasmid, the tetrads were dissected onto YPD solid media (plate A) which was then incubated at 26degreesC for 5-7 days. The colony size at every spore position was noted. In the case of extremely small colonies, the cells were transferred to fresh YPD media (plate B) and incubated for 1 week at 26degreesC. Colonies on YPD were replica plated to SC-URA media with or without G418 to determine genotypes of the colonies.
###end p 22
###begin title 23
Whole cell extract and protein analysis
###end title 23
###begin p 24
###xml 109 113 109 113 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR43">2000</xref>
Cell extracts for protein expression analyses were made using the post-alkaline extraction method (Kushnirov 2000).
###end p 24
###begin p 25
###xml 88 92 88 92 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR59">2001</xref>
###xml 497 499 497 499 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TM</sup>
###xml 615 617 615 617 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TM</sup>
###xml 505 511 <span type="species:ncbi:9986">rabbit</span>
###xml 516 527 <span type="species:ncbi:3704">horseradish</span>
###xml 623 628 <span type="species:ncbi:10090">mouse</span>
###xml 633 644 <span type="species:ncbi:3704">horseradish</span>
Standard Western blotting conditions with SDS-PAGE gels were used (Sambrook and Russell 2001). Protein was transferred to nitrocellulose at 100 mV for 1 h and processed for immunoblotting. Primary antibodies were used in the following concentration: polyclonal anti-Taf14, 1:1,000; monoclonal anti-Flag, M2 (Sigma), 1:1,000; monoclonal anti-H+-vacuolar ATPase (Molecular Probes), 1:5,000; anti-PGK (molecular Probes), 1:5,000; and monoclonal anti-HA, clone HA-7 (Sigma), 1:5,000. The secondary ECLTM anti-rabbit IgG horseradish peroxidase (HRP) labeled antibody (Amersham) was used at 1:5,000, and the secondary ECLTM anti-mouse IgG horseradish peroxidase labeled antibody (Amersham) was used at 1:10,000. The detection of the immobilized proteins conjugated to HRP labeled antibodies was made by using ECL Plus western blotting detection reagents (Amersham-GE Healthcare).
###end p 25
###begin title 26
Immunofluorescence microscopy
###end title 26
###begin p 27
###xml 1245 1248 1214 1215 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">&#174;</sup>
###xml 29 34 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
###xml 1249 1253 <span type="species:ncbi:9925">goat</span>
###xml 1259 1265 <span type="species:ncbi:9986">rabbit</span>
Asynchronous early log phase yeast cells (5 mL) were fixed with 5% formaldehyde (from a 37% stock solution added directly to the culture) for 1 h at 25degreesC on a rotator. Formaldehyde was removed by washing twice with 2 mL 0.1 M potassium phosphate buffer (pH 7.5). Cells were resuspended in 1 mL 0.1 M potassium phosphate buffer (pH 7.5) and 500 muL of cell suspension was permeabilized by the addition of 1 muL 14 M beta-mercaptoethanol and 5.1 muL 5 mg/mL Zymolyase 20T (USB). After digestion at 37degreesC for 35 min, the cells were pelleted, and resuspended in 500 mul 0.1 M potassium phosphate buffer (pH 7.5). Ten microliters of cell suspension was then applied to polylysine coated slides (Fisher) and air dried. After rehydrating by immersion in 1x PBS for 5 min, cells were fixed for 6 min in methanol and 30 s in acetone, both at -20degreesC. Cells were blocked with BLOTTO [1x phosphate buffered saline (PBS), 0.05% Tween-20, 5% nonfat dry milk] for 15 min at room temperature. Affinity purified anti-Taf14 antibody (kind gift of David Drubin, UC Berkeley) was added in BLOTTO at a 1:50 dilution. Incubations continued for 12-16 h at 4degreesC in a humidified chamber. After washing twice with BLOTTO, 15 min each, Alexa Fluor 488(R) goat anti-rabbit IgG (H + L) (Molecular Probes) was added in BLOTTO at a 1:200 dilution. Incubations continued for 1 h at 25degreesC in the dark in a humidified chamber. Slides were washed twice for 15 min each in PBS. Slides were mounted in DABCO mounting medium (2.5% w/v DABCO, Sigma #D-2522, 50 mM Tris pH 7.5, 90% glycerol) containing 2 mug/mL DAPI. Coverslips were sealed with nail polish and stored at 4degreesC in the dark.
###end p 27
###begin title 28
Inmunoprecipitation
###end title 28
###begin p 29
###xml 62 65 62 65 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TMP</sup>
###xml 117 121 117 121 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR71">2003</xref>
###xml 427 430 427 430 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PGK</italic>
N-terminal TAP-tagged Taf14 constructs were made with GatewayPTMP Cloning technology (Invitrogen) (Van Mullem et al. 2003). Three entry clones were generated containing the Taf14 constructs: Taf14 (1-244), taf14[CT] (113-244) and taf14[YEATS] (6-113), following the manufacturer's instructions. From each clone, the ORF was subcloned into the high copy plasmid pVV223 creating N-terminal TAP-tagged expression clones under the PGK promoter.
###end p 29
###begin p 30
###xml 264 269 264 269 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Taf14</italic>
###xml 358 361 358 361 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
###xml 469 470 469 470 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 473 474 473 474 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 477 478 477 478 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 489 490 489 490 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 492 493 492 493 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">4</sub>
###xml 496 497 496 497 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 643 645 643 645 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 712 713 712 713 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2</sub>
###xml 1087 1088 1083 1084 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">g</italic>
###xml 1190 1191 1180 1181 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">g</italic>
###xml 1266 1268 1255 1257 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
###xml 1445 1447 1428 1430 <sup xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">2+</sup>
Genomic N-terminal FLAG tagged Taf2, Sth1, Sas3, Ino80, Tfg1, Taf1 and genomic N-terminal HA-tagged Snf5 (strains: W303-A-Snf5, Ino80-FLAG, W303-A-Sth1, W303-A-Sas3, W303-A-Tfg1, W303-A-Taf1 and W303-A-Taf2) were transformed with the three different truncated TAP-Taf14 mutants. The strains were grown in 150 mL SE-URA/ClonNAT/G418 to log phase. Next, 150 OD600 units were harvested (~0.4 g pellet). The cell pellet was washed in 10 mL of cold PBS (130 mM NaCl, 7 mM Na2HPO4-2H2O, 3 mM NaH2PO4-2H2O adjusted to pH 7.0), pelleted again, and resuspended in 1 mL PBS. After another centrifugation, the cell pellet was resuspended in 0.6 mL TAP-Ca2+-Buffer [50 mM Tris-Cl pH 7.5, 200 mM NaCl, 1.5 mM MgAc, 2 mM CaClB2, 0.15% NP40, 1 mM DTT and one mini protease inhibitor tablet (Roche) per 5 mL], and the tube was filled with acid-washed glass beads (425-600 microm, Sigma). The cells were disrupted mechanically using a bead beater (BioSpec Products, Bartlesville, Oklahoma, USA) 5 times for 20 s with 1-min steps on ice in between. The glass beads were removed by centrifugation at 250 x g. The unbroken cells and the insoluble material were removed by centrifugation at 4degreesC, 20,000 x g for 20 min. The supernatant of each sample was mixed with 50 muL of TAP-Ca2+-Buffer equilibrated Calmodulin Affinity Resin (Stratagene) and incubated with rotating at 4degreesC for 60 min. The beads were pelleted and washed four times with 0.6 mL TAP-Ca2+-Buffer, rotating for 10 min during each wash. They were then resuspended in 100 muL 2x SDS sample buffer (50 mM Tris-Cl pH 6.8, 2% SDS, 10% glycerol, 0.025% bromophenol blue) and resolved on a 7.5% SDS-polyacrylamide gel. After transferring to a nitrocellulose membrane, the gel was immunoblotted with monoclonal antibody against FLAG or HA peptides, and a polyclonal antibody against Taf14 as described above under protein analysis.
###end p 30

Results

To dissect the functional regions of Taf14 and decipher the role of its N-terminal YEATS domain, we created several deletion mutants (Fig. 1b). All the mutants were expressed on a plasmid under TAF14's own promoter and regulatory sequences in a taf14 disruption strain. The expression of all mutant proteins was similar to wild type except for the taf14[YEATS] construct which was expressed or detected poorly (Fig. 1c, left panel). Despite this detection problem, there is no doubt of its expression as the phenotypes for this mutant are completely different than the phenotypes for the taf14 deletion mutant (see below). The cellular localization of all the constructs was also studied and while the taf14[CT] and the taf14[-NT] proteins were located in the nucleus as was the wild-type protein (Fig. 2), taf14[YEATS] was practically undetectable with the Taf14 antibody (Fig. 2). This result is consistent with its low detection by Western blots (Fig. 1c, left panel).Fig. 2Fluorescence microscopy of taf14 mutants' intracellular localization. taf14Delta cells containing different plasmids expressing Taf14 constructs under its natural promoter were fixed and immunostained with a polyclonal Taf14 antibody (left panels, green). The center panels show nuclei in DAPI stained cells (blue). The right panels show the result of merging the immunostaining and the DAPI staining. Pictures represent a single image plane following deconvolution. Cells contained the wild-type Taf14 (top panel); or the taf14[CT] construct (second panel); or the taf14[-NT] construct (third panel); or the taf14[YEATS] construct (fourth panel); or the empty vector pCP3 with the 5' and 3' UTR of Taf14 as a negative control to determine the background (bottom panel)

###xml 687 1381 677 1337 <table-wrap xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Tab3"><label>Table&#160;3</label><caption><p textid="36">Doubling times and lag times prior to entering into exponential phase</p></caption><table frame="hsides" rules="groups"><thead><tr><th align="left">Strain</th><th align="left">Doubling time (h)</th><th align="left">Lag phase (h)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td align="left">CMKy70/pJS02 (<italic>TAF14</italic>)</td><td align="left">2.60&#160;&#177;&#160;0.04</td><td align="left">8.00&#160;&#177;&#160;0.50</td></tr><tr><td align="left">CMKy70/pCP5 (<italic>taf14[CT]</italic>)</td><td align="left">2.97&#160;&#177;&#160;0.18</td><td align="left">7.56&#160;&#177;&#160;0.20</td></tr><tr><td align="left">CMKy70/pCP6 (<italic>taf14[-NT]</italic>)</td><td align="left">3.90&#160;&#177;&#160;0.72</td><td align="left">12.58&#160;&#177;&#160;2.01</td></tr><tr><td align="left">CMKy70/pJS06 (<italic>taf14[-NT</italic><sub><italic>W81A/F84A</italic></sub><italic>]</italic>)</td><td align="left">3.44&#160;&#177;&#160;0.28</td><td align="left">14.15&#160;&#177;&#160;2.23</td></tr><tr><td align="left">CMKy70/pCP4 (<italic>taf14[YEATS]</italic>)</td><td align="left">7.46&#160;&#177;&#160;1.95</td><td align="left">34.60&#160;&#177;&#160;11.13</td></tr><tr><td align="left">CMKy70/pJS06-A (<italic>taf14[YEATS</italic><sub><italic>W81A/F84A</italic></sub><italic>]</italic>)</td><td align="left">9.60&#160;&#177;&#160;2.36</td><td align="left">32.14&#160;&#177;&#160;8.01</td></tr><tr><td align="left">CMKy70</td><td align="left">4.59&#160;&#177;&#160;1.00</td><td align="left">12.54&#160;&#177;&#160;2.45</td></tr></tbody></table><table-wrap-foot><p textid="37">All the liquid cultures were started at OD<sub>600</sub> 0.05 from cells in stationary phase and grown at 30&#176;C in SC-URA, except for CMKy70 that was grown in SC media. Values are the average from at least four different cultures</p></table-wrap-foot></table-wrap>
In synthetic media lacking uracil (SC-URA) at 30degreesC, cells expressing the Taf14 construct missing the complete YEATS domain (taf14[CT]) had a growth rate comparable to cells expressing wild-type Taf14. In contrast, cells expressing the YEATS domain alone (taf14[YEATS]) or a construct with partial deletion of the YEATS domain (taf14[-NT]) showed a diminished growth rate (Table 3). In the case of cells expressing taf14[YEATS], the doubling time was 1.6 times slower than the taf14Delta mutant (CMKy70) and 2.8 times slower than wild-type cells. In addition, the lag phase of cells expressing taf14[YEATS] was more than four times longer than that of the wild-type cells (Table 3).Table 3Doubling times and lag times prior to entering into exponential phaseStrainDoubling time (h)Lag phase (h)CMKy70/pJS02 (TAF14)2.60 +/- 0.048.00 +/- 0.50CMKy70/pCP5 (taf14[CT])2.97 +/- 0.187.56 +/- 0.20CMKy70/pCP6 (taf14[-NT])3.90 +/- 0.7212.58 +/- 2.01CMKy70/pJS06 (taf14[-NTW81A/F84A])3.44 +/- 0.2814.15 +/- 2.23CMKy70/pCP4 (taf14[YEATS])7.46 +/- 1.9534.60 +/- 11.13CMKy70/pJS06-A (taf14[YEATSW81A/F84A])9.60 +/- 2.3632.14 +/- 8.01CMKy704.59 +/- 1.0012.54 +/- 2.45All the liquid cultures were started at OD600 0.05 from cells in stationary phase and grown at 30degreesC in SC-URA, except for CMKy70 that was grown in SC media. The Taf14 deletion constructs were further tested for their ability to complement the temperature sensitivity of a taf14 disruption strain CMKy70 (Table 4, left panel). Taf14[CT] grew as well as wild type at 36degreesC (and at 24degreesC). In contrast, taf14[YEATS] could not complement the temperature sensitivity at 36degreesC. Interestingly, taf14[-NT] could partially complement the growth at 36degreesC as it did during growth at 30degreesC cited above. ###xml 1219 1656 1207 1644 <fig xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" id="Fig3"><label>Fig.&#160;3</label><caption><p textid="43">Growth rates of the different taf14 truncated mutants. Since a taf14 deletion strain is sensitive to different genotoxic stressors, we also tested the effect of the constructs on cell growth under different stress conditions (Table 4; Fig. 3) at both permissive (24degreesC) and non-permissive (36degreesC) temperatures. As expected from the described results above, taf14[CT] showed no growth disadvantage compared to the wild-type strain. Unexpectedly however, it displayed a growth advantage under certain conditions. This advantage was pronounced in the presence of the microtubule destabilizing drug benomyl, as well as on formamide or caffeine at 36C. A less pronounced advantage was seen in the presence of the nucleotide depleting drug 6-azauracil (6-AU). In contrast, under all tested conditions taf14[-NT] allowed very slow or no growth while taf14[YEATS] could not complement the growth defects of the taf14 disruption mutant (Table 4). We conclude that under a wide range of conditions, the YEATS domain of Taf14 limits cell growth both alone and in the context of the wild-type protein. These results may also indicate that in wild-type cells, a negative function of the YEATS domain is being regulated. This possibility is further explored in the "Discussion".Fig. 3Growth rates of the different taf14 truncated mutants. ###end p 42

###begin title 44
Amino acids W81 and W84 form part of a putative regulator interaction site on the YEATS domain surface
###end title 44
###begin p 45
###xml 219 229 219 229 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14[-NT]</italic>
Given the possible regulatory role of the YEATS domain in the cell cycle, we decided to perform a mutagenic scan of the domain to localize sites within the YEATS domain that were important for its function. We used the taf14[-NT] construct since its sensitized phenotype allowed for the observation of mutations that resulted in either increased or decreased growth.
###end p 45

Knowing that charged amino acids are usually necessary for protein-protein interactions, ten different single, double, or triple alanine substitutions of charged amino acid residues were made in the taf14[-NT] construct (Fig. 4a; Table 1). In addition, we created five different alanine substitutions of highly conserved residues hoping to target an essential docking site (Fig. 4a; Table 1). We compared the growth of cells transformed with each of the mutants to that of the strain expressing taf14[-NT] on solid media. Fourteen of the mutants grew similarly to cells harbouring taf14[-NT] (data not shown). However, the double mutant W81A/F84A improved growth (Fig. 4b). In fact, the growth of this mutant became comparable to that of cells expressing the full length protein at 24, 30 and 37degreesC. W81A/F84A also showed slightly better growth than unmutagenized taf14[-NT] on both sorbitol and benomyl (data not shown). There was no significant difference between the protein levels of any of these taf14[-NT] mutants (Fig. S2), arguing against the fact that altered expression levels were responsible for the observed phenotypes. Furthermore, the conserved residues W81 and F84 are predicted to lie on the surface of Taf14 (confirmed by the prediction programs: 3D-PSSM and JNet Secondary Structure Prediction, and Wang et al. 2009) suggesting that they are not serving a structural role, but instead are involved in binding interactions between Taf14 and other protein regulators.Fig. 4a Multiple sequence alignment of the carboxy terminal half of the YEATS domain of Taf14 (61-113 aa) compared with other YEATS family proteins. Conserved residues in bold type. The point mutations used in this study are marked below Taf14; the grey boxes correspond to aminoacids 81 and 84. b Quantitative growth comparison of cells with Taf14 mutant constructs. CMY70 (taf14Delta) strains containing either pJS02 (TAF14), pCP5 (taf14[CT]), pCP6 (taf14[-NT]), pJS06 (taf14[-NTW81A-F84A]), pCP4 (taf14[YEATS]) or pJS06-A (taf14[YEATSW81A-F84A]) were grown to exponential phase (OD600 of 0.8-2.0) and 10x serial dilution were plated onto SC-URA. Cells were grown at 24degreesC and after 3 days the plate was photographed
###end p 46

###xml 531 540 527 536 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">W81A-F84A</italic>
###xml 531 540 527 536 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"><italic>W81A-F84A</italic></sub>
###xml 540 541 536 537 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">]</italic>
###xml 578 581 574 577 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">600</sub>
a Multiple sequence alignment of the carboxy terminal half of the YEATS domain of Taf14 (61-113 aa) compared with other YEATS family proteins. Conserved residues in bold type. The point mutations used in this study are marked below Taf14; the grey boxes correspond to aminoacids 81 and 84. b Quantitative growth comparison of cells with Taf14 mutant constructs. CMY70 (taf14Delta) strains containing either pJS02 (TAF14), pCP5 (taf14[CT]), pCP6 (taf14[-NT]), pJS06 (taf14[-NTW81A-F84A]), pCP4 (taf14[YEATS]) or pJS06-A (taf14[YEATSW81A-F84A]) were grown to exponential phase (OD600 of 0.8-2.0) and 10x serial dilution were plated onto SC-URA. Cells were grown at 24degreesC and after 3 days the plate was photographed
###end p 47
###begin p 48
###xml 121 122 121 122 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab3" ref-type="table">3</xref>
Interestingly, the same double mutant slightly decreased growth when incorporated into the YEATS construct itself (Table 3) and had no impact on growth in either liquid or on solid media when inserted into the wild-type Taf14 protein. These results suggest that the putative YEATS domain interaction site extends beyond these two residues, perhaps along the fragment of the YEATS domain missing in the taf14[-NT] mutant.
###end p 48
###begin title 49
###xml 97 107 97 103 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;</italic>
###xml 112 121 108 113 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dst1&#916;</italic>
Taf14 residues in the C-terminal domain are important for the synthetic lethal phenotype between taf14Delta and dst1Delta
###end title 49

Although TAF14 is not essential, its deletion shows a synthetic lethality with deletion of DST1, the Taf14 protein is therefore required for the survival of cells lacking the transcript elongation factor TFIIS (encoded by DST1) (Fish et al. 2006). To determine if the YEATS domain of Taf14 is required for this genetic interaction, we analyzed the different mutants of TAF14 for their ability to complement the synthetic lethality between taf14Delta and dst1Delta. Plasmids containing the taf14 deletion mutants (Fig. 1b) were transformed into the heterozygous TAF14/taf14Delta::kanr, dst1Delta::hisG/dst1Delta::hisG diploid strain and transformants were sporulated at 30degreesC (Fig. 5). The taf14[YEATS] construct did not complement the synthetic lethality of the double deletion mutant (Fig. 5b), and taf14[-NT] grew slowly, taking 5-7 days for the germinating spores to make visible colonies (Fig. 5c). However, taf14[CT] complemented to equal levels as the wild-type protein (Fig. 5d).Fig. 5Efficiency of different Taf14 constructs in complementing the synthetic lethality between dst1Delta and taf14Delta. Heterozygous diploids were transformed with plasmids containing the indicated Taf14 constructs. Tetrad analysis was carried out with each strain. Grey boxes represent the taf14Delta/dst1Delta double mutant. The taf14::kanr spores were identified by their G418r phenotype. The presence of the URA3 marked plasmid was determined by testing growth on SC-URA media. White boxes represent the dst1Delta/TAF14 mutant. Boxes with X did not have spores. a Tetrad analysis following sporulation of CMKy91 containing pJS02 (TAF14). The tetrads were dissected onto YPD medium, and the plate was photographed after 2 days at 24degreesC. b Similar to a with plasmid pCP4 (taf14[YEATS]). Plates were photographed after 3 days at 24degreesC. c Similar to a with plasmid pCP6 (taf14[-NT]). Plates were photographed after 7 days at 24degreesC. d Similar to a with plasmid pCP5 (taf14[CT]). Plates were photographed after 3 days at 24degreesC. Even after 9 days, no growth was detectable in the blank spots
###end p 50

###begin p 52
###xml 112 131 112 123 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;dst1&#916;</italic>
###xml 190 200 182 188 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;</italic>
###xml 218 219 206 207 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>
###xml 245 264 233 244 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;dst1&#916;</italic>
###xml 413 423 387 397 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14[-NT]</italic>
We also studied the ability of taf14[CT] or taf14[-NT] mutants to complement the temperature sensitivity in the taf14Deltadst1Delta background (conferred by the absence of Taf14). As in the taf14Delta background (Fig. 3, left bottom panel), the taf14Deltadst1Delta strain expressing the taf14[CT] construct grew as well as the strain expressing the wild-type construct at 36degreesC. But the cells containing the taf14[-NT] construct grew poorly although this protein was able to complement the temperature sensitivity.
###end p 52
###begin p 53
###xml 47 57 47 57 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14[-NT]</italic>
###xml 65 66 65 66 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab1" ref-type="table">1</xref>
###xml 114 124 114 120 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;</italic>
###xml 129 138 125 130 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">dst1&#916;</italic>
###xml 145 146 137 138 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>

We next tested the ability of point mutants in taf14[-NT] (Table 1) to complement the synthetic lethality between taf14Delta and dst1Delta (Fig. 6). The double mutant W81A/F84A conferred a growth advantage in the taf14Deltadst1Delta cells, as it did in the taf14 null background (Fig. 4b). Surprisingly, in contrast to our result in the taf14 null background, three additional mutations outside the YEATS domain conferred a growth advantage in the taf14Deltadst1Delta background: K124A, E129A/E130A, and K133A, suggesting that these three C-terminal residues are particularly relevant for the TAF14 and DST1 genetic interaction. Thus, we concluded that genetic interaction between TAF14 and DST1 is not dependent on the YEATS domain of Taf14 either.Fig. 6Complementation of the taf14Delta dst1Delta synthetic lethality by the taf14[-NT*] point mutants. Cells were grown at 24degreesC for 3 days. Mutants that could rescue the taf14[-NT] slow growth phenotype are marked with arrows. The slight growth advantage seen for the H103A/D104A mutant in this figure was not reproducible

###begin title 55
Taf14 interaction with proteins related to transcription and chromatin
###end title 55
###begin p 56
To determine the region of Taf14 protein required for the physical interaction of Taf14 with RSC, Swi/Snf, Ino80, NuA3, TFIIF and TFIID, we performed small-scale interaction assays.
###end p 56
###begin p 57
###xml 210 214 210 214 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR24">2001</xref>
###xml 270 274 270 274 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">1998</xref>
###xml 357 361 357 361 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR33">2000</xref>
###xml 375 379 375 379 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR31">2002</xref>
###xml 452 457 452 457 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR63">2003a</xref>
###xml 459 460 459 460 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR64">b</xref>
###xml 573 577 573 577 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">2006</xref>
###xml 597 601 597 601 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR35">2008</xref>
###xml 699 703 699 703 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR48">1996</xref>
###xml 776 780 776 780 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR70">1997</xref>
###xml 876 880 876 880 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR72">1997</xref>
###xml 898 902 898 902 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR47">1998</xref>
Proteins from each complex were FLAG- or HA-tagged in their chromosomal loci. Tagged proteins included: (1) Snf5, thought to coordinate the assembly and nucleosome-remodeling activities of Swi/Snf (Geng et al. 2001); (2) Sth1, the DNA-dependent ATPase of RSC (Du et al. 1998); (3) Sas3, the histone acetyl transferase catalytic subunit of NuA3 (John et al. 2000; Howe et al. 2002); (4) Ino80, the DNA-dependent ATPase of the Ino80 complex (Shen et al. 2003a, b); (5) Tfg1, the largest subunit of TFIIF that functions in transcription initiation and elongation (Fish et al. 2006; Khaperskyy et al. 2008); (6) Taf1, the largest subunit of TFIID, with histone acetyltransferase activity (Mizzen et al. 1996) and involved in promoter binding and G1/S progression (Tansey and Herr 1997); (7) Taf2, also a TFIID subunit, stabilizes TFIID binding to the core promoter (Walker et al. 1997; Martinez et al. 1998). The addition of the tag to these proteins did not affect the growth of these strains, suggesting that the tag did not impair function (data not shown).
###end p 57
###begin p 58
###xml 100 103 100 103 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">PGK</italic>

###xml 531 532 531 532 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig7" ref-type="fig">7</xref>
###xml 534 540 534 540 <label xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Fig.&#160;7</label>
###xml 657 789 657 765 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;/FLAG-Tfg1, taf14&#916;/FLAG-Sas3, taf14&#916;/FLAG-Taf1, taf14&#916;/FLAG-Taf2, taf14&#916;/HA-Snf5, and taf14&#916;/FLAG-Sth1</italic>

Full length Taf14, taf14[CT] and taf14[YEATS] were TAP-tagged and expressed from plasmids under the PGK promoter (Fig. 1c, right panel). Each strain carrying one of the seven FLAG- or HA-tagged proteins was transformed with each of the three different Taf14-encoding plasmids. The incorporation of the Taf14 constructs into the different nuclear complexes was tested by immunoprecipitation experiments using the TAP tag on Taf14, followed by Western blotting with FLAG or HA antibodies to detect the protein in the complexes (Fig. 7).Fig. 7Interaction assay between Taf14 and different nuclear complexes. ###end p 58
###begin p 59
###xml 117 249 117 225 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;/FLAG-Tfg1, taf14&#916;/FLAG-Sas3, taf14&#916;/FLAG-Taf1, taf14&#916;/FLAG-Taf2, taf14&#916;/HA-Snf5, and taf14&#916;/FLAG-Sth1</italic>
###xml 417 438 393 414 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Sec2" ref-type="sec">Materials and methods</xref>
###xml 660 672 636 648 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">black arrows</italic>
###xml 675 684 651 660 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">Red stars</italic>
Interaction assay between Taf14 and different nuclear complexes. The same amount of soluble protein from extracts of taf14Delta/FLAG-Tfg1, taf14Delta/FLAG-Sas3, taf14Delta/FLAG-Taf1, taf14Delta/FLAG-Taf2, taf14Delta/HA-Snf5, and taf14Delta/FLAG-Sth1 strains containing plasmids expressing either TAP-tagged wild-type Taf14 (pJS40), TAP-taf14[CT] (pJS41), or the TAP tag alone (pVV223) were processed as described in "Materials and methods". The Western-blotted membranes were incubated with anti-FLAG M2 or anti-HA to visualize the protein representing each complex. The protein A present in the TAP tag allowed for the visualization of all versions of Taf14 (black arrows). Red stars mark the FLAG and HA-tagged proteins. (CT) represents the TAP-taf14[CT]. (FL) represents the TAP-Taf14. + indicates a cell extract of that strain without expression of Taf14 and was used as a positive control. - represents the negative control expressing the TAP tag alone
###end p 59
###begin p 60
###xml 107 108 107 108 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig7" ref-type="fig">7</xref>
Both full length Taf14 and mutant taf14[CT] were associated with Swi/Snf, RSC, NuA3, TFIIF and TFIID (Fig. 7). We could draw no conclusions about the association with Ino80 as it could not be clearly detected in any of the five pull down experiments performed. Additionally, in the case of Swi/Snf, the Taf14[CT] was not always well incorporated and sometimes taf14[CT] appeared to immunoprecipitate more Taf2 than the full length protein itself. These results prevented us from making quantitative conclusions about the associations. Taf14[YEATS] was convincingly (three out of three experiments performed, data not shown) associated with Taf1, one of the TFIID components tested. However, in general the TAP-taf14[YEATS] peptide did not immunoprecipitate successfully and was not readily detected in association with the tested proteins even when the polyclonal anti-Taf14 antibody was used to maximize detection of the YEATS domain. Nevertheless, it is clear that the YEATS domain is not essential for Taf14's association with Swi/Snf, RSC, NuA3, TFIIF or TFIID.
###end p 60
###begin title 61
Discussion
###end title 61
###begin p 62
###xml 398 402 398 402 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">1994</xref>
###xml 416 420 416 420 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR56">1995</xref>
###xml 436 440 436 440 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR5">1996</xref>
###xml 452 456 452 456 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">1998</xref>
###xml 470 475 470 475 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR63">2003a</xref>
###xml 477 478 477 478 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR64">b</xref>
###xml 494 498 494 498 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR34">2005</xref>
###xml 693 697 693 697 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR75">1993</xref>
###xml 712 716 712 716 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">1994</xref>
###xml 738 742 738 742 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR37">2004</xref>
The work presented here explores the cellular function of Taf14 and studies the role of its conserved YEATS domain by structure-function and mutational analysis of various regions of Taf14. This protein is a member of at least six nuclear proteins complexes that are involved in transcription and chromatin modification (RSC, SWI/SNF, Ino80, NuA3, TFIIF, TFIID and possibly Mediator) (Henry et al. 1994; Poon et al. 1995; Cairns et al. 1996; Du et al. 1998; Shen et al. 2003a, b; Kabani et al. 2005). Its deletion renders cells sensitive to high temperature and osmolarity, causes deficiency in both constitutive and induced transcription and leads to altered actin cytoskeleton (Welch et al. 1993; Henry et al. 1994; Kimura and Ishihama 2004). The associations of Taf14 with different complexes and the resulting phenotypes of the disruption strain indicate that this protein might facilitate many nuclear processes in the cell, or that it links different processes to a general common mechanism. Exploring the cellular function of Taf14 and determining the role of its conserved YEATS domain was the focus of this work. We show that the YEATS domain has a negative role in cell growth, and it is not needed to bind the nuclear complexes that contain Taf14 nor to complement the Taf14 synthetic lethality with TFIIS when both proteins are absent from the cell.
###end p 62
###begin p 63
###xml 3 16 3 16 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">S. cerevisiae</italic>
###xml 168 172 168 172 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR77">2004</xref>
###xml 512 517 512 517 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14</italic>
###xml 592 601 592 601 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">advantage</italic>
###xml 667 668 667 668 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab3" ref-type="table">3</xref>
###xml 675 676 675 676 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>
###xml 1231 1232 1231 1232 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig7" ref-type="fig">7</xref>
###xml 1467 1471 1467 1471 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR77">2004</xref>
###xml 1635 1639 1635 1639 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR53">2006</xref>
###xml 3 16 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">S. cerevisiae</span>
###xml 1586 1591 <span type="species:ncbi:9606">human</span>
In S. cerevisiae, there are three proteins that contain the YEATS domain (Taf14, Yaf9, Sas5), and cells require at least one of these proteins to survive (Zhang et al. 2004). For this reason, when Taf14 deletion mutations in the YEATS domain were constructed, the initial expectation was that the YEATS domain would stimulate cell growth. However, contrary to expectations, we found that in all the cases tested, the C-terminal domain of Taf14 (taf14[CT]) was sufficient to complement all of the phenotypes of a taf14 deletion strain. Furthermore, the YEATS domain deletion provides a growth advantage compared to wild-type cells under a variety of conditions (Table 3; Fig. 3). In addition, a deletion of just the first 61 amino acids in the Taf14 YEATS domain (the taf14[-NT] construct) creates a strain with a growth disadvantage compared to a full deletion of the YEATS domain (aa 6-113, the taf14[CT] construct). These results, coupled with the extremely slow growth of cells expressing the YEATS domain alone, strongly suggest that the YEATS domain of Taf14 has an important negative regulatory function. We show that the YEATS domain is not needed for the association of Taf14 with any of the tested nuclear complexes (Fig. 7). This is consistent with the observation that only the coiled-coil C-terminal domain of Yaf9 is required for its interaction with Esa1, the histone acetyltransferase catalytic subunit of, NuA4, and not its YEATS domain (Zhang et al. 2004). In the same way, the non-YEATS domain (also a coiled-coil region) of GAS41 is required for interaction with the human TIP60 and SRCAP complexes (Park and Roeder 2006). Together, these observations suggest that the hypothesized negative effect of the YEATS domain would not be transmitted via the complexes themselves. While it is still formally possible that there may be interactions between Taf14 and other subunits of these complexes that are affected by deletion of the YEATS domain, we prefer a model where the YEATS domain functions outside of the complexes themselves, possibly by binding and modifying growth regulators through a specific binding site on its surface.
###end p 63
###begin p 64
###xml 215 216 215 216 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig4" ref-type="fig">4</xref>
###xml 704 710 701 707 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14,</italic>
Compatible with the possibility that the YEATS domain contains such a regulatory motif, site-specific mutations (W81A, F84A) in the remaining YEATS residues of taf14[-NT] conferred a growth advantage to cells (Fig. 4b). These residues are predicted to reside in one of the loops of a beta-sandwich, likely on the protein surface. Thus, this region might be critical for interaction with a protein or proteins in the cellular pathway in which the YEATS domain of Taf14 plays a role. The growth rescue of this sensitized mutant via these point mutations argues against the possible misfolding of the construct. This increased growth is not observed when the mutations are incorporated into the full length TAF14, but the reason for this result could be that we cannot detect increased growth over that of an already healthy strain. Since the YEATS domain is not required for interaction with at least five of the six complexes in which Taf14 is a subunit, these residues (W81 and F84) are unlikely to be necessary for interaction with the complexes themselves. It seems more likely that these residues are highlighting a regulatory region interacting with other proteins important for Taf14 function. Further, it seems plausible that the YEATS domain is carrying out a function separate from that of the C-terminal domain of Taf14.
###end p 64
###begin p 65
###xml 19 24 19 24 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14</italic>
###xml 79 83 79 83 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DST1</italic>
###xml 110 114 110 114 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR20">2002</xref>
###xml 302 306 302 306 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR39">2005</xref>
###xml 341 346 341 346 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14</italic>
###xml 351 355 351 355 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">DST1</italic>
###xml 468 472 468 472 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR21">2006</xref>
###xml 503 512 503 512 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">W81A-F84A</sub>
###xml 554 573 554 565 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;dst1&#916;</italic>
###xml 622 623 614 615 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig5" ref-type="fig">5</xref>
###xml 625 626 617 618 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>
###xml 765 766 757 758 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig6" ref-type="fig">6</xref>
###xml 938 948 930 936 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;</italic>
###xml 1005 1024 993 1004 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;dst1&#916;</italic>
Another feature of TAF14 is that its disruption makes the gene encoding TFIIS (DST1) essential (Fish and Kane 2002). TFIIS is a transcript elongation factor that allows RNA polymerase II to recover from the backtracked state and read through blocks to elongation such as the nucleosome (Kireeva et al. 2005). The synthetic lethality between TAF14 and DST1 appears to be mediated through TFIIF, both an initiation and an elongation factor in transcription (Fish et al. 2006). Both taf14[CT] and taf14[-NTW81A-F84A] complement the synthetic lethality in a taf14Deltadst1Delta strain as effectively as wild-type Taf14 (Figs. 5, 6). In addition, three other mutants (K124A, E129A/E130A, or K133A) in the taf14[-NT] construct complement as well as wild-type Taf14 (Fig. 6). Interestingly, none of these residues are located in the YEATS domain, and these mutants do not rescue the slow growth phenotype of the taf14[-NT] mutant in the haploid taf14Delta strain. Thus, since these mutant residues do rescue the taf14Deltadst1Delta synthetic lethality, they are residues that are needed specifically for the TFIIS function. This result reinforces the likelihood that the YEATS domain and the C-terminal domain are involved in distinct functions.
###end p 65
###begin p 66
###xml 14 19 14 19 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">TAF14</italic>
###xml 94 98 94 98 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR13">2000</xref>
###xml 191 201 191 197 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;</italic>
###xml 425 430 421 426 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14</italic>
###xml 574 583 570 575 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">snf5&#916;</italic>
###xml 666 670 658 662 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR24">2001</xref>
###xml 686 696 678 684 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;</italic>
###xml 780 784 768 772 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR5">1996</xref>
###xml 857 861 845 849 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR79">2003</xref>
###xml 790 795 <span type="species:ncbi:4932">yeast</span>
Disruption of TAF14 also renders genes encoding subunits of SWI/SNF essential (Davie and Kane 2000), and mutant alleles in the two larger subunits of TFIIF are also synthetically lethal with taf14Delta (unpublished results). It is possible that more of this type of genetic interaction might be found if direct experiments were done with genes in the other Taf14 bearing-complexes as well. Alternatively, the defects seen in taf14 null mutants might be explained by disassembled complexes (one or more) or by assembled but functionally inactive complexes. For example, in a snf5Delta mutant, Swi/Snf is not well assembled leading to an inactive complex (Geng et al. 2001). In contrast, taf14Delta mutants still contain a stable Swi/Snf complex with ATPase activity (Cairns et al. 1996) and yeast TFIIF without Taf14 is still active in vitro (Ziegler et al. 2003). In any case, Taf14's presence in the six nuclear complexes suggests that it might perform a broad function designed to enlist these complexes in particular processes or locations, perhaps at particular times. Clearly Taf14 is not needed in all circumstances as the complexes can function in its absence at the permissive temperature.
###end p 66
###begin p 67
###xml 170 180 170 176 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">taf14&#916;</italic>
###xml 249 253 245 249 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR30">1994</xref>
###xml 338 342 334 338 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR74">1994</xref>
###xml 436 440 432 436 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR17">2008</xref>
###xml 496 506 492 498 <italic xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">cdc40&#916;</italic>
###xml 531 535 523 527 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR12">2004</xref>
###xml 822 826 814 818 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR15">1998</xref>
###xml 842 846 834 838 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR76">2008</xref>
###xml 956 960 948 952 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" ref-type="bibr" rid="CR44">2003</xref>
###xml 1477 1478 1469 1470 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Tab3" ref-type="table">3</xref>
###xml 1584 1585 1576 1577 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">0</sub>
###xml 1595 1596 1587 1588 <sub xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink">1</sub>
###xml 1766 1767 1758 1759 <xref xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" rid="Fig3" ref-type="fig">3</xref>
All these observations, together with several others found in the literature (see below), lead to plausible and testable hypotheses. First, it has been reported that the taf14Delta cells have elongated buds and actin hyperpolarization (Henry et al. 1994), phenotypes often associated with cell cycle regulating proteins (Welch and Drubin 1994). Second, cells lacking Taf14 spend much longer in G1 than do wild-type cells (Erlich et al. 2008). Third, Taf14 is essential for the G2/M transition in cdc40Delta cells (Dahan and Kupiec 2004), important for cell cycle progression. Fourth, Taf14 is related to two YEATS domain containing mammalian proteins Gas41 and AF-9, both implicated in spindle function. Fifth, Taf14 is a subunit of RSC, a chromatin remodeling complex also essential for cell cycle progression (Du et al. 1998; Yamada et al. 2008). Sixth, the YEATS domain containing protein Yaf9 is important for the deposition of H2A.Z (Le Masson et al. 2003), a histone variant required for proper timing of transcription of cell cycle regulatory proteins. Along this line of thought, the results reported here also suggest a role for Taf14 and its YEATS domain during the cell cycle. While the doubling times of cells containing taf14[-NT] and YEATS constructs are longer than for cells containing the normal Taf14 or the taf14[CT] constructs, the lag time prior to starting cell division is profoundly increased, especially for cells containing the YEATS construct (Table 3). This observation suggests a problem in reentering the cell cycle after stationary phase, perhaps from G0 to late G1 where cells commit to DNA replication. Additionally, the taf14[CT] mutant grows better than wild type in the presence of benomyl, a microtubule destabilizing drug (Fig. 3), as if the absence of the YEATS domain actually stabilized nuclear microtubules.
###end p 67
###begin p 68
In summary, expression of only the YEATS domain makes the cells sick: a partial YEATS domain in Taf14 imparts slowed growth; a full deletion of the YEATS domain of Taf14 generates healthy cells. Additional experiments will be necessary to find the YEATS-binding regulator, to test, in a quantitative way, what fraction of each nuclear complex has Taf14 and the mutant versions bound, and to determine the importance of the YEATS domain for Taf14's function in the nucleus.
###end p 68
